<div id="review_23700538" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23700538" />
    <a title="Eli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/891169-eli"><img alt="Eli" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23700538?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eli" href="/user/show/891169-eli">Eli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23700538" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10398751503703716323">Hubert Selby is one of my favorite writers, but I couldn't help but feel somewhat ambivalent about this one. While it still has all of the elements of a good Selby book, by about halfway through the book I couldn't help but feel that it was getting a bit repetitive.<br/><br/>The story is one of a man who may or may not be wrongly imprisoned, and his sadistic and brutal thoughts of revenge on the people who put him in this place. Needless to say, a story like this can't help but become repetitive after a w</span>
  <span id="freeText10398751503703716323" style="display:none">Hubert Selby is one of my favorite writers, but I couldn't help but feel somewhat ambivalent about this one. While it still has all of the elements of a good Selby book, by about halfway through the book I couldn't help but feel that it was getting a bit repetitive.<br /><br />The story is one of a man who may or may not be wrongly imprisoned, and his sadistic and brutal thoughts of revenge on the people who put him in this place. Needless to say, a story like this can't help but become repetitive after a while.<br /><br />Selby mines the depths of human depravity and sadism to get into the head of the narrator, and the result is nothing short of revolting. The narrator's revenge fantasies range from self-aggrandizing courtroom theatrics about his unjust imprisonment to debasing his tormentors to the point where they are nothing more than tortured dogs with no semblance of humanity left in them. Selby has such a flair for the showcasing the worst parts of the human psyche, and uses that talent to disturbing effect in this book.<br /><br />I would definitely recommend it to anyone who is already a fan of Hubert Selby's, but it's not a good introduction to his otherwise stellar oeuvre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10398751503703716323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

Ballard\'s mesmerizing and ferociously prescient novel imagines a terrifying future in which solar radiation and global warming have melted the polar ice caps and Triassic-era jungles have overrun a submerged and tropical London. Set during the year 2145, the novel follows biologist Dr. Robert Kerans and his team of scientists as they confront a surreal cityscape populated by giant iguanas, albino alligators, and endless swarms of malarial insects. Nature has swallowed all but a few remnants of human civilization, and, slowly, Kerans and his companions are transformed—both physically and psychologically—by this prehistoric environment. Echoing Joseph Conrad\'s Heart of Darkness—complete with a mad white hunter and his hordes of native soldiers—this &quot;powerful and beautifully clear&quot; (Brian Aldiss) work becomes a thrilling A cable network in New Orleans wants to put together a paranormal investigation team and have them check out purported haunted locations in Louisiana. For every location with solid filming potential, each member of the team gets a five hundred dollar pay check. And Buggy knows without question that if anyone can sniff out the ‘restless’ dead, it’s Nonie.Knowing nothing about paranormal investigation, but lured by the sound of the adventure and the dollar signs circling through her head, Nonie agrees to become part of the Boo Krewe, the name Buggy has formally given the makeshift team.The lure of money may be one thing, but what Nonie doesn’t realize is that occasionally the dead, so excited that someone can actually see and hear them, decide to follow her home, into her bed—the bathroom—the shower.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">About Hubert Selby Jr.</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1397502493p3/26335.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">Hubert Selby Jr.
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,963
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10277939254890441882">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Dr</span>
  <span id="freeText10277939254890441882" style="display:none">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Drawing from the soul of his Brooklyn neighborhood, he began writing something called "The Queen Is Dead," which evolved, after six years, into his first novel, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> (1964), a book that Allen Ginsberg predicted would "explode like a rusty hellish bombshell over America and still be eagerly read in a hundred years."<br /><br />Selby's second novel, <i>The Room</i> (1971), considered by some to be his masterpiece, received, as Selby said, "the greatest reviews I've ever read in my life," then rapidly vanished leaving barely a trace of its existence. Over the years, however, especially in Europe, <i>The Room</i> has come to be recognized as what Selby himself perceives it to be: the most disturbing book ever written, a book that he himself was unable to read again for twenty years after writing it.<br /><br />"A man obsessed / is a man possessed / by a demon." Thus the defining epigraph of <i>The Demon</i> (1976), a novel that, like <i>The Room</i>, has been better understood and more widely embraced abroad than at home.<br /><br />If <i>The Room</i> is Selby's own favorite among his books, <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> (1978) contains his favorite opening line: "Harry locked his mother in the closet." It is perhaps the truest and most horrific tale of heroin addiction ever written. <br /><br /><i>Song of the Silent Snow</i> (1986) brought together fifteen stories whose writing spanned more than twenty years.<br /><br />Selby continued to write short fiction, screenplays and teleplays at his apartment in West Hollywood. His work appeared in many journals, including <i>Yugen, Black Mountain Review, Evergreen Review, Provincetown Review, Kulchur, New Directions Annual, Swank</i> and <i>Open City</i>. For the last 20 years of his life, Selby taught creative writing as an adjunct professor in the Master of Professional Writing program at the University of Southern California. Selby often wryly noted that The New York Times would not review his books when they were published, but he predicted that they'd print his obituary.<br /><br />The movie <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn,</i> Directed by Uli Edel, was made in 1989 and his 1978 novel <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> was made into a film that was released in 2000. Selby himself had a small role as a prison guard. <br /><br />In the 1980s, Selby made the acquaintance of rock singer Henry Rollins, who had long admired Selby's works and publicly championed them. Rollins not only helped broaden Selby's readership, but also arranged recording sessions and reading tours for Selby. Rollins issued original recordings through his own 2.13.61 publications, and distributed Selby's other works.<br /><br />During the last years of his life, Selby suffered from depression and fits of rage, but was always a caring father and grandfather. The last month of his life Selby spent in and out of the hospital. He died in Highland Park, Los Angeles, California of chronic obstructive pulmonary lung disease. Selby was survived by his wife of 35 years, Suzanne; four children and 11 grandchildren.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10277939254890441882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
